<div id="review_1436976475" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1436976475" />
    <a title="Charles Dee Mitchell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell"><img alt="Charles Dee Mitchell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267455462p2/2272557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1436976475?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Dee Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Dee Mitchell" href="/user/show/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell">Charles Dee Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell?shelf=contemporary-lit">contemporary-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1436976475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18022126580645983512">Leemet is a young man of the forest people. When he was a child too young to remember the experience, his parents had made the move to the village. His father learned to work the fields and even developed a taste for bread, but Leemet’s mother became bored and could not adjust to village life. This made her easy pickings for a bear, those irresistible lotharios notorious for stealing away human wives. Leemet’s father caught his wife and her lover in flagrante delicto, and the startled animal bit</span>
  <span id="freeText18022126580645983512" style="display:none">Leemet is a young man of the forest people. When he was a child too young to remember the experience, his parents had made the move to the village. His father learned to work the fields and even developed a taste for bread, but Leemet’s mother became bored and could not adjust to village life. This made her easy pickings for a bear, those irresistible lotharios notorious for stealing away human wives. Leemet’s father caught his wife and her lover in flagrante delicto, and the startled animal bit his head off. Leemet’s mother, subsequently abandoned by the bear, returned to the forest with her infant son. <br /><br />For Leemet this has been a good thing. His life in the forest is fun and adventurous. His uncle is one of the last fluent speakers of Snakish, the language that allows humans to communicate with snakes, those wisest of forest inhabitants, and exercise control over other animals. Without Snakish, it is difficult to maintain an adequate herd of wolves, and wolves are needed for both transportation and their milk. Leemet masters the ancient language, and he spends his days with human friends his own age, his older male relatives, and the invaluable snakes who offer both advice and the warmth of their burrows in the winter. He is also friendly with the primates, an older hominid species who has not left the trees and spend most of the time breeding wood lice the size of sheep.<br /><br />Andrus Kivirahk is the most popular contemporary author in Estonia, known as a satirical journalist and a bestselling novelist. This novel, which appears to take place in a fantastic version of his homeland during the early middle ages, is his first to be translated into English. It is a thoroughly engaging tale of old ways giving way to modernity, filled with episodes of comic invention, family drama, young love, and the sadness of old traditions giving way to a modernity that offers much but exacts a stiff toll. <br /><br />Leemet is the perfect hero for such a tale, a tenth-century, Estonian Huckleberry Finn. Despite his snakish wisdom, he can be very naïve. He perceives the armored knights that come from across the sea on their armored horses as single, metallic creatures. He is surprised to discover how relatively easy they are to kill. The obese, berobed monks who accompany them he assumes are their ever-pregnant wives.<br /><br />Leemet may be naïve, but he is not stupid like those who have abandoned the forest for the village. Village dwellers, vehement about their newly acquired civilized skills and Christianity, believe all sorts of superstitious nonsense about the forest and its supposedly demonic denizens. And they rejoice in their subservience to their German-speaking masters. The village leader was taken as a young man across the sea for training in civilization and the new religion. When he speaks with pride of his time spent as the bedmate to an archbishop, Leemet cannot help but feel that there is something off about this arrangement.<br /><br />But Kivirahk’s novel will be the story of sad, funny, and inevitable change. As I read it, I wondered what added resonance it had for its Estonian audience, who have taken it so to heart that a popular board games has been created around it. For English readers it is a thoroughly enjoyable historical fantasy, and an introduction to a major European writer.<br /><br />(I received a free advance ebook from Net Galley)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18022126580645983512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1443277320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1443277320" />
    <a title="Muse Monthly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41748795-muse-monthly"><img alt="Muse Monthly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469119277p2/41748795.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1443277320?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Muse Monthly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Muse Monthly" href="/user/show/41748795-muse-monthly">Muse Monthly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1443277320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7542230232530936655">I remember bringing home One Hundred Years of Solitude after reading it for my Realism in Literature class for university. I remember feeling fundamentally <i>changed after</i> journeying through Marquez's fictional Columbia, enchanted by the words on the page, my heart pounding and my imagination running wild. And, as I often do in this case, I remember handing the book off to my mother for her enjoyment. <br/><br/>Her response was: "It's too weird". <br/><br/>And it happens sometimes that I fail to see the weirdness of </span>
  <span id="freeText7542230232530936655" style="display:none">I remember bringing home One Hundred Years of Solitude after reading it for my Realism in Literature class for university. I remember feeling fundamentally <i>changed after</i> journeying through Marquez's fictional Columbia, enchanted by the words on the page, my heart pounding and my imagination running wild. And, as I often do in this case, I remember handing the book off to my mother for her enjoyment. <br /><br />Her response was: "It's too weird". <br /><br />And it happens sometimes that I fail to see the weirdness of some novels, that my obsession with fantastical fiction has blurred the line of what is acceptable and not, what is progressive and experimental, what is strange or bizzare or uncomfortable. There are a lot of topics in literature that no longer bother me - sex, violence, supernatural, you name it. Doesn't bother me at all. <br /><br />But.<br /><br />The Man Who Spoke Snakish is weird.<br /><br />It really is fantastically weird, everything from bear fucking to giant insects to limbless grandfathers with razor-sharp teeth, The Man Who Spoke Snakish hits the ground running, weaving the story of Leemet, a boy raised in the forest of Estonia, who becomes the symbol and protector of The Old Ways. I say "The Old Ways" because we all know what that means - at some point in all cultures, modernity swoops in and begins to erase everything that was once held dear, everything that people know and cherish. Some embrace change, and in this case, move into a village and change their names to fit the new standards. However, some cling to The Old Ways, finding themselves keepers of tradition, and often, watching the world change around them with distaste and uncertainty. Lemeet is one such person, who is staunch in his fight against the oncoming wave of newness. He is proud of his heritage, proud of the simplicity of forest life, and especially proud of his ability to speak Snakish - a quickly dying language that allows him to communicate and have mastery over animals. <br /><br />But among the weirdness and the violence of the story, there is a clear and culturally relevant narrative. The majority of the book deals with impending change and how we deal with inevitability. Do we give up The Old Ways, pack up, and move to the village? Do we change our names, and with that, change the people we are? Or do we hold tight to our principals, to the things we know, and cement our identity in them? There are consequences in either decision, and a struggle that results in a palpable cultural tension. The meat of this book is the discussion of old versus new, and the weight of being the last of your kind.<br /><br />The Man Who Spoke Snakish reads like folklore, reads like a story that has been passed down from generation to generation. It is complex and epic, wonderful and yes, weird. But at the center of all that weirdness is a tale of morality, humanity, and our relationship with nature. And blood. Lots of blood. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7542230232530936655" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2312540131?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/26764370-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2312540131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12882116421687673312">I found this book in the Alternative History category on CloudLibrary. I can’t argue with that, as it appears to be set in the times of the Christianisation of Estonia - the Northern Crusades of the 13th century - but it surely didn’t happen quite like this.<br/><br/>That section is right below the Absurdist section, and as I read the book, I misremembered that as being the category. Nothing in the book made me think it might not be. Not the wolf dairy, not the hissing at animals, the kissing of bears, or</span>
  <span id="freeText12882116421687673312" style="display:none">I found this book in the Alternative History category on CloudLibrary. I can’t argue with that, as it appears to be set in the times of the Christianisation of Estonia - the Northern Crusades of the 13th century - but it surely didn’t happen quite like this.<br /><br />That section is right below the Absurdist section, and as I read the book, I misremembered that as being the category. Nothing in the book made me think it might not be. Not the wolf dairy, not the hissing at animals, the kissing of bears, or the naked tree climbing.<br /><br />A bit slow moving in parts, it gets going towards the end, particularly after a rescue scene I totally didn’t expect. If this book was a movie, that scene would have the audience standing cheering.<br /><br />It was made clear throughout the book that there could be no happy ending, but the ending was unexpectedly satisfying and complete. I learned quite a bit about Estonia, but only by googling things from the book. It could have done with a glossary to explain things.<br /><br />It’s tempting to see this book as merely a criticism of Christianity. It is critical of it - the naive beliefs, the blind trust in god, and even clerical pedophilia. But I wonder if it’s also a criticism of the belief that everything was better “in the good old days”. You remember the good old days, when we could talk to animals, and tell them to wait while we killed them for food? Things were so much easier then.<br /><br />Parental warnings: paganism, mild sexual references, bestiality, arboreal nudity and voyeurism, joyous, slashing violence.</span>

<div id="review_1414754374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1414754374" />
    <a title="Colleen ~ The Clever Girl from Gallifrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20565655-colleen-the-clever-girl-from-gallifrey"><img alt="Colleen ~ The Clever Girl from Gallifrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569597212p2/20565655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1414754374?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colleen ~ The Clever Girl from Gallifrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colleen ~ The Clever Girl from Gallifrey" href="/user/show/20565655-colleen-the-clever-girl-from-gallifrey">Colleen ~ The Clever Girl from Gallifrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27227863-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20565655-colleen-the-clever-girl-from-gallifrey?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20565655-colleen-the-clever-girl-from-gallifrey?shelf=fantasy-world">fantasy-world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1414754374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3110855398084023903">**I received a copy of this book from the publisher via NetGalley in exchange for an honest review**<br/><br/><i>The Man Who Spoke Snakish</i> is an adult fantasy tale. I stress the “adult” part of that description because it has its moments of violence and gore, as well as coarse language. It was an adult fairy tale and I loved it so much. The book is set in medieval Estonia and takes place in both a forest and a nearby village. The main character, Leemet, lives in the forest and follows the old ways of those w</span>
  <span id="freeText3110855398084023903" style="display:none">**I received a copy of this book from the publisher via NetGalley in exchange for an honest review**<br /><br /><i>The Man Who Spoke Snakish</i> is an adult fantasy tale. I stress the “adult” part of that description because it has its moments of violence and gore, as well as coarse language. It was an adult fairy tale and I loved it so much. The book is set in medieval Estonia and takes place in both a forest and a nearby village. The main character, Leemet, lives in the forest and follows the old ways of those who can speak a language known as Snakish, which allows them to communicate with snakes and all other animals. But the world is changing, and those of the forest are leaving to settle in villages where they begin to worship God and look up to the Iron Men (knights). Leemet watches as everything around him changes and those of the forest are left to fight to preserve their way of life, and to prevent the old ways from being lost to civilization, religion, and ignorance. <br /><br />This story was highly fantastical and imaginative. You become completely immersed in a world where there are wondrous creatures and beautiful landscapes, all portrayed through rich descriptions. As you read you feel like you are part of the story and the old world, and it is such an enchanting feeling. But for all the beauty in this novel there is also much sadness and you are forced to feel the pain of the characters. This combination of beauty and pain makes for such a powerful story and saturates the reader with emotion and passion. <i>The Man Who spoke Snakish</i> is a stunning tale that will stick with you long after you have finished reading it.<br /><br />As much of a fantasy story <i>The Man Who Spoke Snakish</i> is, it is also very deep and tackles some very critical subjects. The book is considered ecocriticism, where it shows and analyzes environmental issues and concerns, as well as the treatment of nature. Throughout the story we see the “progression” of people from being in tune with nature, to no longer understanding nature as they switch to a societal life. Then there are the animals, who go from living in harmony with humans to being frightened humans. Animals and people transition from understanding one another and working together to fearing and bringing harm upon one another. This draws a good parallel to the direction the world has taken where instead of humans living in unity with nature we have begun to take it for granted and abuse it. The book shows this change among species and way of life so perfectly and in a very profound manner. <br /><br />Another major component of this book is the subject of religion and falling into the beliefs of the masses, forgoing your own heart and mind to follow others. Depending on how you read this book it can be viewed as religious criticism, but I feel like it is done in an intelligent way, full of intensity, and not in a means where it bashes religion. This aspect also ties into the subject matter prevalent through the book of the dangers of over-civilization and the creation of societies that are indoctrinated to live their lives a certain way and believe only the things they are told. This is such a powerful message that really pertains to the world today and the direction that humankind is headed in.<br /><br />One area where I feel like this book suffered had nothing to do with the story itself, but I think had more to do with the translation into English. I know translations are never easy and in many cases the story is affected by it, which was an occasional issue with the English version of <i>The Man Who Spoke Snakish</i>. I think some of the phrasing was not effectively translated and also there was an overuse of language at times which made the story seem a little wordy. The main problem this created for me was that the story felt a little dragged out at times. I do not want to fault a novel at all based on the translation, but it is hard to view it any other way when I cannot read the original text. I was torn between four and five stars, and I think if I had been able to read the original this would be a five star book for me; I kind of want to say I give it five stars for story and four stars for translation.<br /><br />If I tried to express all my feelings and my full analysis of this book my review would be several pages long, so there are a lot of things I must leave out, unfortunately. What I will say though is that this may be an Estonian novel, but it is something that every culture and country could understand and learn from. The book uses a beautiful and savage fairy tale to act as a vessel for some very important issues in the world today and it definitely is a story that everyone could learn from. <i>The Man Who Spoke Snakish</i> is not a story that can be taken lightly; it is brutal at times, highly opinionated, and heartbreaking, yet always beautiful and imaginative.<br /><br /><i>“At that moment it seemed to me that we knew perhaps too much that others didn’t know, and on the other hand too little that was known to everyone else…”</i><br /><br /><b>Check out this review and others on <a href="http://doublelettersbookclub.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Double Letters Book Club</a></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3110855398084023903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1435120387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1435120387" />
    <a title="Marzie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2475000-marzie"><img alt="Marzie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605373892p2/2475000.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1435120387?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marzie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marzie" href="/user/show/2475000-marzie">Marzie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1435120387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8058744447926229842">I have such mixed feelings about this book, which is quite unique and which juxtaposes organized religious beliefs with empirical knowledge, modernized agrarian community versus that in a more hunter/gatherer forest-dwelling lifestyle that is in touch with nature and less driven by appearances. The clash of cultures is interesting in the first part of the book but starts to feel very heavy-handed, if not brutal, in the second half. (I'm not saying the brutal clash between paganism and the Cathol</span>
  <span id="freeText8058744447926229842" style="display:none">I have such mixed feelings about this book, which is quite unique and which juxtaposes organized religious beliefs with empirical knowledge, modernized agrarian community versus that in a more hunter/gatherer forest-dwelling lifestyle that is in touch with nature and less driven by appearances. The clash of cultures is interesting in the first part of the book but starts to feel very heavy-handed, if not brutal, in the second half. (I'm not saying the brutal clash between paganism and the Catholic church of the Middle Ages is not accurate!)<br /><br />A number of female characters (Salme, Hiie, Magdaleena, Leemet's mother) in the book annoyed me. Other than the mostly genderless Ints, and the female primate, all the female characters were largely stereotypical and quite sexualized, serving little purpose other than as nagging food providers or as potential sex partners, whether for Leemet, bears or knights. The use of the temptation of the agrarian lifestyle, offerings of bland bread, and the Catholic church was amusing, as in Leemet's world, bread seems to take the place of the proverbial apple and Eve is replaced, in no small irony, by Magdaleena. The Village is certainly no Garden of Eden, nor is the forest.<br /><br />I enjoyed the early portions of the story about Leemet's childhood and youth, loved his camaraderie with Ints, but found the second half of the book to be incredibly brutal. I didn't always like Leemet as he grew older, and there was little to like about many of the characters other than perhaps Ints. Whether viewed as an allegory about the potential foolish reasoning and hypocrisy of any faith, or as a Bildungsroman about Leemet's coming of age and learning to believe only in what he can see, I found the pace uneven and the language sometimes awkward. I am not sure it's not the fault of the translation, though, which sometimes was languid and other times choppy.<br /><br />This book definitely is not a happy folktale and readers should go into it for the immersion experience of what is offered as a bit of Estonian folk culture, not expecting a warm and charming story. Definitely, not a story for the kiddies. It's a very sad and sometimes violent story, of loneliness and of isolation, for virtually all of the forest dwelling characters. But some parts of the book are unique and truly memorable. It's worth a read. I can also recommend the audiobook, which is well-produced, for those who might stumble on the pronunciation of Estonian names like Leemet and Vootele.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8058744447926229842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2082671238" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30337728-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558247206p2/30337728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2082671238?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/30337728-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2082671238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6177487392660198375"><b> King Arthur: Look you stupid bastard, I've cut off both your arms. Black Night: 'Tis just a flesh wound you chicken, now fight!"</b> <i> John Cleese, Monty Python</i><br/><br/>Well I'm about as shallow as a dingo's pee puddle, and initially, I found this story very similar to the famous Black Night scene from Monty Python and the Holy Grail...only funnier. People in the bad old days of Estonian Folklore seemed to be coming to gory endings all the time. But more than that; apparently it's OK if your sister bonks a b</span>
  <span id="freeText6177487392660198375" style="display:none"><b> King Arthur: Look you stupid bastard, I've cut off both your arms. Black Night: 'Tis just a flesh wound you chicken, now fight!"</b> <i> John Cleese, Monty Python</i><br /><br />Well I'm about as shallow as a dingo's pee puddle, and initially, I found this story very similar to the famous Black Night scene from Monty Python and the Holy Grail...only funnier. People in the bad old days of Estonian Folklore seemed to be coming to gory endings all the time. But more than that; apparently it's OK if your sister bonks a bear, as long as she marries him. Or to have wolf's milk on your Weeties in the morning. Or that you speak to snakes in their own language (actually, I could use that talent out on the Keperra Golf Course where I frequently have snake encounters). Or that you have a grandfather who has had both legs chopped off (Monty Python ripoff) and yet can build himself a set of wings (out of human bones no less) to wreak deadly vengeance from above on the knights who "delegged" him. <br /><br />Well - actually - the whole story is an allegory (or an "all a gory") for the rapid change that faced Estonia in the middle ages. You know...things like religion, and bread, and cow's milk. Aggh! Too much change. (See - I'm not so shallow after all.) I guess the author got his message across, but I didn't really appreciate his fanatical anti-religious sentiment which appeared on almost every page.<br /><br />The story line is about as smooth as a saw edge and the characters are just plain boring, or stupid, or both. There's a lot that got lost in the translation methinks. BUT... if you like Monty Python, you'll LOL at this story; many times. My absolute favorite character, by far, is Leemet's mom, whose answer to any family crisis is FOOD! What's that dear? Your wife just got her neck bit by a wolf? Never mind - eat this haunch of venison, and have seconds. What's that? You just killed the bishop and his four knights? Oooo...that's big. Better eat this whole roast goat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6177487392660198375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2299030557" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2299030557" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2299030557?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=estonian-lit">estonian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2299030557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15607947542735039097">I might have given ‘The Man Who Spoke Snakish’ four stars had I not read it after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30209230.Speaking_of_Universities' title='Speaking of Universities by Stefan Collini' rel='nofollow noopener'>Speaking of Universities</a>, hoping for something to cheer me up. Despite an intermittently light and parodic tone, this is ultimately a dark, fatalistic, and depressing novel, full of arbitrary violence and cruelty. In form and themes, it reminded me a lot of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24694092.Laurus' title='Laurus by Evgenij Vodolazkin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Laurus</a>, but is if anything harsher. The forest setting is wonderfully evoked, as are its strange inhabitants. I particularly liked Pirre and Raak the Primates, </span>
  <span id="freeText15607947542735039097" style="display:none">I might have given ‘The Man Who Spoke Snakish’ four stars had I not read it after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30209230.Speaking_of_Universities" title="Speaking of Universities by Stefan Collini" rel="nofollow noopener">Speaking of Universities</a>, hoping for something to cheer me up. Despite an intermittently light and parodic tone, this is ultimately a dark, fatalistic, and depressing novel, full of arbitrary violence and cruelty. In form and themes, it reminded me a lot of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24694092.Laurus" title="Laurus by Evgenij Vodolazkin" rel="nofollow noopener">Laurus</a>, but is if anything harsher. The forest setting is wonderfully evoked, as are its strange inhabitants. I particularly liked Pirre and Raak the Primates, Ints the adder, and Leemut the narrator’s extraordinary family. He recounts his tumultuous life in the forest, as his friends and neighbours move to a village and become subsistence farmers rather than hunter-gatherers. He is continually aware of being the last of his community, so even moments of triumph or levity have a pall cast over them. The titular Snakish is a language that allows him to converse with and command animals, with results that vary from charming to horrific. Perhaps something was lost in translation, or I’d need knowledge of Estonian history that I lack, but I couldn’t work out what more the story was than an ambivalent elegy for abandoned forms of life. Although forest existence isn’t glamourised, village life is mocked and criticised mercilessly. The loss of the Snakish language and other aspects of forest life are mourned as wasteful and senseless, yet the reasons for their waning aren’t really explored. ‘The Man Who Spoke Snakish’ includes a lot of memorable images, some of which I loved and some of which I’d rather forget. It’s an strange and violent story of abandoning hope.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15607947542735039097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1318424146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1318424146" />
    <a title="Maxine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5208303-maxine"><img alt="Maxine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333118765p2/5208303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1318424146?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxine" href="/user/show/5208303-maxine">Maxine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1318424146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4685193656558936233">Leemet lives with his family in the forest and it provides them with everything they need –shelter, food, clothing. They ride and milk wolves, share living space with intelligent adders and lecherous bears, and hang out with ancient primates who keep lice for pets. And they speak Snakish, an ancient language from the time when men and snakes were brothers and which allows them to converse as well as control most animals, all except insects (except a few like lice). But almost everyone else has m</span>
  <span id="freeText4685193656558936233" style="display:none">Leemet lives with his family in the forest and it provides them with everything they need –shelter, food, clothing. They ride and milk wolves, share living space with intelligent adders and lecherous bears, and hang out with ancient primates who keep lice for pets. And they speak Snakish, an ancient language from the time when men and snakes were brothers and which allows them to converse as well as control most animals, all except insects (except a few like lice). But almost everyone else has moved to the village where they eat bread for which they must till the fields and domesticate the animals, where they have converted to Christianity and obey the knights and monks, and where they are no longer able to speak snakish. <br /><br />Set in Medieval Estonia, The Man Who Spoke Snakish by author Andrus Kivirahk is a very hard book to categorize. It seems in parts like realist fantasy while I wondered if the author based at least some of it perhaps on Estonian folk tales. I was so intrigued to know more about the author and the book (which, trust me, means I liked it a whole lot), I learned that it is considered ‘the first Estonian eco-novel’ by many and, by others, an allegory for the problems small nations like Estonia are facing in a changing world. <br /><br />However you describe this novel, it is a completely unique story full of strange creatures, odd characters, and plenty of black humour. It is hugely entertaining and more than a little addicting and, for fans of fantasy who are looking for something completely different, this is one fun read. One caveat though: there’s a great deal of violence so, if you’re squeamish, this may not be for you. For anyone else, I can’t recommend it highly enough.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4685193656558936233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1520316944" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1520316944" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51813793-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453444373p2/51813793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1520316944?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/51813793-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1520316944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1550140522128713290">This story charts the life of Leemet as his world dies around him. Much of his childhood feels like an old fairy tale. However, the world is changing and modernizing. The fairy tales pass away, either by being revealed as frauds or being abandoned. <br/><br/>The writing in this book is stronger than I'd expected given the rumors of poor translation. The style is straightforward, but descriptive. You will not have trouble visualizing the world Leemet occupies. The plot is likewise straightforward--Leemet g</span>
  <span id="freeText1550140522128713290" style="display:none">This story charts the life of Leemet as his world dies around him. Much of his childhood feels like an old fairy tale. However, the world is changing and modernizing. The fairy tales pass away, either by being revealed as frauds or being abandoned. <br /><br />The writing in this book is stronger than I'd expected given the rumors of poor translation. The style is straightforward, but descriptive. You will not have trouble visualizing the world Leemet occupies. The plot is likewise straightforward--Leemet grows up in a way of life that is dying out. And though the book does have one or two surprises to toss your way, there are no big twists.<br /><br />What is compelling, though, is the ongoing tension between keeping the old ways alive and being lonely or giving into the new ways and having companionship. Though he tries, Leemet cannot straddle the two. Leemet also must struggle with the knowledge that he is the very last. He clearly wishes this responsibility away from himself, but at the same time cannot let go of superior ways of old. <br /><br />However, this is not easily about how modernization and foreign ways destroy native customs and weaken native populations. The books also demonstrate how clinging to the past in either ignorance (as with Hiie's father) or in reverence (as with the Primates) is also problematic. The world has moved on and no longer needs the same things it used to and has found new, more efficient ways to do other things. But, does avoiding remaining stuck and decaying in the past require abandoning your own culture, knowledge, and beliefs?<br /><br />Willful ignorance, in its many forms, is the true villain in this book. (And Leemet is at his most villainous late in the book when he refuses to listen to some bears).<br /><br />Overall I liked this book and I like the questions it casually posed. If you're interested in the transitions between cultures/beliefs/times, this is definitely a good book to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1550140522128713290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond"><img alt="Bryn Hammond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523356367p2/7224461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2327704511?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryn Hammond" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond">Bryn Hammond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802124128" href="/book/show/25246592-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=imagined-fiction">imagined-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2327704511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11130004901962595683">Not what I expected: more comic effect and fantasy, less anchor in anthropology (the forest people herd and milk wolves, and strangely despise animals). I felt I didn't get the book for most of it. Forager life seemed almost as unattractively portrayed as village life -- involving as much 'stupidity' -- and the hero holds out for a personal fate more self-determined than either. Culminates in a celebratory comic violence in an anti-modern crusade, along with nostalgia for simple gender. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=audiobook-edition">audiobook-edition</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=2018-reading-challenge">2018-reading-challenge</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=eesti">eesti</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=estonian-language">estonian-language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=estonia">estonia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789615-alan-teder?shelf=translated-from-estonian">translated-from-estonian</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1473472855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11415075157338974530"><b>Audiobook edition brilliantly read by Aaron Landon</b><br/><br/>I read and reviewed the paperback edition of Christopher Moseley's translation when it was first published in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1482055629' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Wild Fairy Tale for Adults</a>. I grabbed a download of the audiobook at the same time but had kept it in reserve until recently.<br/><br/>Aaron Landon's narration performance only enhanced my enjoyment of this English translation further. Landon does a very thorough job of giving each of the characters their own voice. This was especially entertaini</span>
  <span id="freeText11415075157338974530" style="display:none"><b>Audiobook edition brilliantly read by Aaron Landon</b><br /><br />I read and reviewed the paperback edition of Christopher Moseley's translation when it was first published in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1482055629" rel="nofollow noopener">A Wild Fairy Tale for Adults</a>. I grabbed a download of the audiobook at the same time but had kept it in reserve until recently.<br /><br />Aaron Landon's narration performance only enhanced my enjoyment of this English translation further. Landon does a very thorough job of giving each of the characters their own voice. This was especially entertaining in the sibilant voicing of the snake characters such as Ints and their extended family. The lustful bear Mõmmi gets somewhat of a mixed French / Russian accent which I couldn't quite place, but was enjoyable never the less. All in all, this book is probably now my absolutely favourite English translation of any Estonian novel.<br /><br />To the best of my knowledge, this is still the only audiobook edition of a translated Estonian novel in existence to date as of late 2018. There are audiobooks of parts of Sofi Oksanen's Estonian Quartet, but since those were first written in Finnish they can't be counted for their subject matter alone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11415075157338974530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2258558018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2258558018" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43525878-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2258558018?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mia" href="/user/show/43525878-mia">Mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781511372770" href="/book/show/27429769-the-man-who-spoke-snakish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2258558018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4273328300616587515">I loved this book. It was such an amazing mix of sadness and joy at the same time. But I do fear, that someone who isn't Estonian, may not see everything that the book has to offer. I am Estonian, and read this book both in Estonian and then also in English, and my honest opinion is that the book was better in Estonian. When I read it, I got caught up in the moment, even though I relate to these characters and events in no way. Leemet is a mysterious character and I love that even when you are f</span>
  <span id="freeText4273328300616587515" style="display:none">I loved this book. It was such an amazing mix of sadness and joy at the same time. But I do fear, that someone who isn't Estonian, may not see everything that the book has to offer. I am Estonian, and read this book both in Estonian and then also in English, and my honest opinion is that the book was better in Estonian. When I read it, I got caught up in the moment, even though I relate to these characters and events in no way. Leemet is a mysterious character and I love that even when you are finished with the book, there still is mysteries that there weren’t many answers for. This lets the reader imagine some aspects of the book and in a book like this, a lot is possible so the options are endless. <br />The book does an amazing job describing the settings and people in a way that you don’t know too much, but you know enough to imagine the world that the characters live in. This book is sad more than it is happy, and I was surprised that I love this book as much as I do. I usually stir away from books which are sad and tragic, and since I am Estonian, I knew before starting the book, that it had many tragic elements. But they did surprise me, the tragic element was very unexpected and I couldn't put the book down if there was a risk of something tragic happening. <br />For an Estonian, this book is a symbol. It means more to someone who can read it in the native language, but I think even in English, this book needs to be read. It has been my favorite book for years, and I hope others will love it just as much.<br />And at the risk of giving any spoilers, don't get attached to ANY character in the book. Just friendly advice. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4273328300616587515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1114645156266193782">This is an excellent book with quite unexpected and unique plot which has the connection to the Estonian culture and history. The tale is intense and keeps on reading, but at the same time it is a philosophical and thought-provoking story of contemporary social perspective. I would definitely recommend it for all readers</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('r1RSUCfeA4tKwA3hqI2Q8gJQVl6HVEkPlfyXnYtTNos5w0+Wpw5v97TY0rN6o0FqvC97ypUye8n2AKNqbjcwuQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BvIWF+cYFY5yn+W4ZYvgCh7Id1SVUKg6gdrPxkZYYZuQZQvRZ8h58oyHOuq3pTGSoLdawIc2mvziJvsxozxnqQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/337614.Andrus_Kivir_hk">About Andrus Kivirähk</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/337614.Andrus_Kivir_hk"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1337250660p3/337614.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/337614.Andrus_Kivir_hk">Andrus Kivirähk

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14977916328112044504">Andrus Kivirähk is an Estonian journalist, playwright and novelist. His writing style can be called self-mocking and sarcastic with dark humour. His best known work "Rehepapp ehk November", a.k.a. "Rehepapp", has been translated to Finnish and Norwegian. "Mees, kes teadis ussisõnu", a bestseller in Estonia, so popular that a board-game was based on it, has been translated to English as "The Man Wh</span>
  <span id="freeText14977916328112044504" style="display:none">Andrus Kivirähk is an Estonian journalist, playwright and novelist. His writing style can be called self-mocking and sarcastic with dark humour. His best known work "Rehepapp ehk November", a.k.a. "Rehepapp", has been translated to Finnish and Norwegian. "Mees, kes teadis ussisõnu", a bestseller in Estonia, so popular that a board-game was based on it, has been translated to English as "The Man Who Spoke Snakish". These books, as well as his other historical-themed works such as "Ivan Orava mälestused" and "Kalevipoeg" resonated strongly with contemporary Estonian society.<br /><br />Kivirähk is also the author of the children's book "Leiutajateküla Lotte" and its sequels, and wrote the screenplay for the cartoon based on it.<br /><br />Andrus Kivirähk works as a journalist, and is married with 3 children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14977916328112044504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
